<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome sequencing and phylogenetic analysis of allotetraploid Salix matsudana Koidz"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-12-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Polyploidy is a common phenomenon among willow species. In this study, genome sequencing was conducted for Salix matsudana Koidz (also named Chinese willow), an important greening and arbor tree species, and the genome of this species was compared with those of four other tree species in Salicaceae. The total genome sequence of S. matsudana was 655.72&#8201;Mb in size, with repeated sequences accounting for 45.97% of the total length. In total, 531.43&#8201;Mb of the genome sequence could be mapped onto 38 chromosomes using the published genetic map as a reference. The genome of S. matsudana could be divided into two groups, the A and B genomes, through homology analysis with the genome of Populus trichocarpa, and the A and B genomes contained 23,985 and 25,107 genes, respectively. 4DTv combined transposon analysis predicted that allotetraploidy in S. matsudana appeared ~4 million years ago. The results from this study will help reveal the evolutionary history of S. matsudana and lay a genetic basis for its breeding."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Polyploidy is a common phenomenon among willow species. In this study, genome sequencing was conducted for <i>Salix matsudana</i> Koidz (also named Chinese willow), an important greening and arbor tree species, and the genome of this species was compared with those of four other tree species in Salicaceae. The total genome sequence of <i>S. matsudana</i> was 655.72âMb in size, with repeated sequences accounting for 45.97% of the total length. In total, 531.43âMb of the genome sequence could be mapped onto 38 chromosomes using the published genetic map as a reference. The genome of <i>S. matsudana</i> could be divided into two groups, the A and B genomes, through homology analysis with the genome of <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, and the A and B genomes contained 23,985 and 25,107 genes, respectively. 4DTv combined transposon analysis predicted that allotetraploidy in <i>S. matsudana</i> appeared ~4 million years ago. The results from this study will help reveal the evolutionary history of <i>S. matsudana</i> and lay a genetic basis for its breeding.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Willows, the generic term for tree species in <i>Salix</i>. L and <i>Chosenia</i> Nakai of Salicaceae, include deciduous arbors and shrubs mainly distributed in temperate and frigid zones of the Northern Hemisphere. Willow tree species play important roles in energy production, afforestation, and greening. To date, over 520 willow tree species have been discovered worldwide. Asia is the center of origin for willow, and 257 willow tree species are distributed in China<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Gullberg, U. Towards making willows pilot species for coppicing production. For. Chron. 69, 721â726 (1993)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d32769e595">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Lindegaard, K. N. &amp; Barker, J. Breeding willows for biomass. Asp. Appl Biol. 49, 155â162 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d32769e598">2</a></sup>. Willow tree species are resistant to a number of stresses, including salt stress<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Zhang, J. et al. The genetic architecture of growth traits in Salix matsudana under salt stress. Hortic. Res. 4, 17024 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d32769e602">3</a></sup>, drought stress<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Polt, P., SjÃ¶din, P., Weih, M., RÃ¶nnberg-WÃ¤stljung, A. C. &amp; Berlin, S. Genome-wide transcriptional and physiological responses to drought stress in leaves and roots of two willow genotypes. BMC Plant Biol. 15, 244 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d32769e606">4</a></sup>, water stress<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Hangs, R. D., Schoenau, J. J., Van Rees, K. &amp; Steppuhn, H. Examining the salt tolerance of willow (Salix spp.) bioenergy species for use on salt-affected agricultural lands. Can. J. Plant Sci. 91, 509â517 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d32769e611">5</a></sup>, and heavy metal stress<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Yanitch, A. et al. Transcriptomic response of purple willow (Salix purpurea) to arsenic stress. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 1115 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d32769e615">6</a></sup>, and provide important functions in ecological restoration<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Aronsson, P., Dahlin, T. &amp; Dimitriou, I. Treatment of landfill leachate by irrigation of willow coppiceâplant response and treatment efficiency. Environ. Pollut. 158, 795â804 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d32769e619">7</a></sup>.</p><p>The genetic background of willow is complex. Chromosome ploidy, including diploidy (2<i>n</i>â=â12xâ=â38), triploidy, tetraploidy, pentaploidy, hexaploidy, and even dodecaploidy (2<i>n</i>â=â12xâ=â228), is abundant among willow species in nature<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Suda, Y. &amp; Argus, G. W. Chromosome numbers of some North American Salix. Brittonia 20, 191â197 (1968)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d32769e632">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Macalpine, W. J., Shield, I. F., Trybush, S. O., Hayes, C. M. &amp; Karp, A. Overcoming barriers to crossing in willow (Salix spp.) breeding. Asp. Appl. Biol. 90, 173â180 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d32769e635">9</a></sup>. Most shrub willow tree species are diploid, while most arbor species are allopolyploid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Argus, G. W. Infrageneric classification of Salix (Salicaceae) in the new world. Syst. Bot. Monogr. 52, 1â121 (1997)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d32769e639">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Barcaccia, G., Meneghetti, S., Albertini, E., Triest, L. &amp; Lucchin, M. Linkage mapping in tetraploid willows: segregation of molecular markers and estimation of linkage phases support an allotetraploid structure for Salix albaÃ Salix fragilis interspecific hybrids. Heredity 90, 169â180 (2003)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d32769e639_1">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Barcaccia, G., Meneghetti, S., Lucchin, M. &amp; De Jong, H. Genetic segregation and genomic hybridization patterns support an allotetraploid structure and disomic inheritance for Salix species. Diversity 6, 633â651 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d32769e642">12</a></sup>. To provide the necessary genetic characteristics of willow for scientific study, the genomes of several diploid shrub willow species, including <i>Salix suchowensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Dai, X. et al. The willow genome and divergent evolution from poplar after the common genome duplication. Cell Res. 24, 1274â1277 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d32769e648">13</a></sup> and <i>Salix purpurea</i> (available at <a href="http://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov">http://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov</a>), were sequenced successively.</p><p><i>Salix matsudana</i> Koidz is an allotetraploid arbor tree species in <i>Salix</i> with high stress resistance and strong adaptability. <i>S. matsudana</i> has been cultivated widely in China, and has thus been given the name âChinese willowâ. <i>S. matsudana</i> has been introduced to many areas around the world, including Australia, Europe, and North America<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Qiao, G. et al. Comparative proteomic analysis of responses to salt stress in Chinese willow (Salix matsudana Koidz). Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 32, 814â827 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d32769e677">14</a></sup>. Selection and breeding of new stress-resistant varieties of <i>S. matsudana</i> have been conducted at several institutions, and a series of elite varieties, including â9901â and âHailiu 1â, were selected and applied in coastal regions of China. To better understand the molecular characteristics of <i>S. matsudana</i>, the first draft genome of <i>S. matsudana</i> was assembled, and the phylogenetic characteristics of <i>S. matsudana</i> were revealed by comparing its genome with available genomes of <i>S. suchowensis</i>, <i>S. purpurea</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d32769e703">15</a></sup>, and <i>Populus euphratica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Ma, T. et al. Genomic insights into salt adaptation in a desert poplar. Nat. Commun. 4, 1â9 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d32769e709">16</a></sup> in the same family.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and quality assessment</h3><p>According to the kmer distribution, the heterozygosity rate of <i>S. matsudana</i> was estimated to be 0.71% (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#MOESM1">S1</a>), demonstrating the highly heterozygous genome of this species. In total, 78.50âG of raw data with a depth of 125Ã and Q30 of &gt;93.11% was obtained by the second-generation Illumina sequencing platform (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#MOESM1">S1</a>); 37.58âG of raw data with a total base content of 35.64âGb, a mean length of 10.31âKb, and a mean read quality of 0.834 were obtained by the third-generation PacBio sequencing platform (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#MOESM1">S2</a>), and 289âG of optical data were obtained based on standard protocols. The raw data from the third-generation PacBio sequencing platform were initially assembled using Canu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722â736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d32769e737">17</a></sup> and Quickmerge<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Chakraborty, M. et al. Contiguous and accurate de novo assembly of metazoan genomes with modest long read coverage. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, e147 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d32769e742">18</a></sup> software, followed by error correction with Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Chin, C. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050â1054 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d32769e746">19</a></sup> software using the second-generation sequencing data, and final scaffold extension with Irys-scaffolding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Jennifer, S. &amp; NicHerndon. Irys-Scaffolding: Irys-scaffolding Tools Version 1.0.0 &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.5281/zenodo.18591&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d32769e750">20</a></sup> software using the optical data. Finally, a genome of <i>S. matsudana</i> with a total size of 655.72âMb was obtained, for which the number of scaffolds, scaffold N50, contig N50, and GC content were 2120, ~12.35âMb, ~945.75âKb, and 33.55%, respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#Tab1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary of the <i>S. matsudana</i> genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>After quality control of the assembled sequences, 450 core genes accounting for 98.25 of the total (458; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#MOESM1">S3</a>) and 1313 complete core genes accounting for 94.38 of the total (1440; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#MOESM1">S4</a>) were identified in the CEMGA (Core Eukaryotic Genes Mapping Approach) database and BUSCO database, respectively, demonstrating the acceptable consecutiveness, coverage rate, and accuracy of the <i>S. matsudana</i> genome constructed in this study.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome annotation</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec5">Analysis of repeated sequences</h4><p>A repeated sequence database of the <i>S. matsudana</i> genome was constructed based on the principles of structure prediction and de novo sequencing. The genome of <i>S. matsudana</i> was clustered and ranked according to a previously published genetic map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Zhang, J. et al. A high-density genetic map of tetraploid Salix matsudana using specific length amplified fragment sequencing (SLAF-seq). PLoS ONE 11, e0157777 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d32769e969">21</a></sup>. In total, 531.43âMb of the genome sequences (accounting for 81.43%) was mapped onto the 38 chromosomes of <i>S. matsudana</i>, the length of which ranged from 8.80â28.42âMb. In total, 300.41âMb of repeated sequences, accounting for 45.97% of the whole genome, was identified, among which the numbers of SSRs and unknown repeated sequences were 6185 and 116,059, accounting for 0.51% and 6.76% of the total genome, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#MOESM1">S5</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Gene prediction</h3><p>A total of 57,841 genes with a total length of 216,437âKb, a mean exon length of 229âbp, a mean intron length of 350âbp, and a density of 108.9 genes per Mb were predicted. In addition, 220 miRNAs (belonging to 27 gene families), 321 rRNAs (belonging to 4 gene families), 1557 tRNAs (belonging to 23 gene families), and 3494 pseudogenes were discovered (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#Fig1">1</a>). The results from blast alignment showed that 99.49% of the genes were annotated in the NR, COG, and KEGG databases (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#MOESM1">S6</a>), while only 295 genes were not annotated in these databases.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Circular graph of the S. matsudana genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Circular graph of the <i>S. matsudana</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Comparisons between genomes of willow and poplar</h3><p>Comparisons of genome sequences between <i>S. matsudana</i> and four other species in Salicaceae, namely, <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, <i>P. euphratica</i>, <i>S. suchowensis</i>, and <i>S. purpurea</i>, showed that the total number of genes in <i>S. matsudana</i> were 1.53 times and 2.17 times that in <i>S. purpurea</i> and <i>S. suchowensis</i>, respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#Tab2">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Comparisons between the genomes of five tree species in Salicaceae</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A total of 50,381 of the 57,841 genes in the <i>S. matsudana</i> genome could be clustered into 18,233 gene families, among which 13,787 were common among the five species of Salicaceae, while 302 were specific to <i>S. matsudana</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#MOESM1">S7</a> and Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#Fig2">2</a>). A total of 793 genes were included in the gene families specific to <i>S. matsudana</i>, and their functions were related to metabolic process, cellular process, response to stimulus, and biological regulation (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#MOESM1">S2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="The common and specific gene families in the genomes of five tree species in Salicaceae."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: The common and specific gene families in the genomes of five tree species in <i>Salicaceae</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00424-8/MediaObjects/41438_2020_424_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc"
