/></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The numbers under the five tree species represent cluster gene number and total gene family number, respecitively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Phylogenetic analysis of <i>S. matsudana</i>
</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec9">Expansion analysis of genes in <i>S. matsudana</i>
</h4><p>Further investigation of the expansion and contraction of gene families in the five tree species in Salicaceae demonstrated that the numbers of gene families and genes that were expanded in <i>S. matsudana</i> were 3128 and 20,699, respectively (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#MOESM1">S3</a>). The functions of these genes were mainly related to metabolic process, cellular process, cell part, cell binding, catalytic activity, and so on. In addition, a series of root development-related gene families, such as root cap, root hair defective 3 GTP-binding protein (<i>RHD3</i>), and transcription factor regulating root and shoot growth via Pin3, were identified as expanded families, and their gene numbers were expanded to 18, 27 and 29, respectively.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec10">Fourfold degenerate site (4DTv) analysis of the five tree species in Salicaceae</h4><p>The duplication time of the genome was estimated by comparing the 4DTv mutation rates among genes in the collinear fragments of the five tree species in Salicaceae (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#Fig3">3</a>). A genome-wide replication event was predicted to have occurred 220â358 million years ago (MYA) among the ancestors of Salicaceae, since the first peak was at 0.48 and the synonymous mutation rates (<i>R</i> values) of Salicaceae ranged from 1.09âÃâ10<sup>â9</sup> to 0.67âÃâ10<sup>â9</sup>/site/year<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Dai, X. et al. The willow genome and divergent evolution from poplar after the common genome duplication. Cell Res. 24, 1274â1277 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1431">13</a></sup>. Similarly, a second genome-wide replication event was predicted to have occurred 36.7â59.7âMYA among species in Salicaceae, since the second peak was at 0.08, at which time differentiation between poplar and willow was estimated to have appeared. In addition, the allotetraploid was estimated to have formed 4.6â7.5âMYA in <i>S. matsudana</i>, since a peak appeared at 0.01 for this species.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="The 4DTv distribution among the five tree species in Salicaceae."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: The 4DTv distribution among the five tree species in <i>Salicaceae</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00424-8/MediaObjects/41438_2020_424_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00424-8/MediaObjects/41438_2020_424_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="456" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>The x-coordinates and y-coordinates represent 4DTv distance and percentage of syntenic genes, respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec11">Long terminal repeat sequence analysis among the five tree species in Salicaceae</h4><p>Among the long terminal repeat sequence (LTR) transposons in <i>S. matsudana</i>, 213,911, with a total length of 93,513,152âbp and accounting for 14.31% of the repeated sequences, were Copia transposons, while 227,569, with a total length of 100,397,354âbp and accounting for 15.36% of the repeated sequences, were Gypsy transposons.</p><p>After estimation of the two types of LTRs by sequence bifurcation analysis, it was found that the insertion of LTR transposons into Salicaceae genomes began 6âMYA, while the insertion of LTR transposons began 4âMYA in the <i>S. matsudana</i> genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#Fig4">4</a>), further confirming that the formation of allotetraploid <i>S. matsudana</i> occurred recently.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Insertion time estimation for LTR transposons in the five tree species in Salicaceae."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Insertion time estimation for LTR transposons in the five tree species in Salicaceae.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00424-8/MediaObjects/41438_2020_424_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00424-8/MediaObjects/41438_2020_424_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="509" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>The x-coordinates and y-coordinates represent the age of LTRs and number of intact LTR, respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec12">Expression analysis of root-related genes in arbor and shrub willows</h4><p>Arbor and shrub willows are diverse in terms of tiller number, plant height, and primary root development. For instance, the primary roots are well developed in arbor willow, while the primary roots are not obvious, but the lateral roots are well developed in shrub willow. A series of genes were identified as rapidly evolving genes and annotated as root-related genes after comparing the two shrub willow genomes with that of <i>S. matsudana</i>. The expression levels of these genes were determined in ten arbor and shrub willow varieties, using <i>SmACTIN1</i> as a reference gene. The results showed that <i>TCTP1</i> and <i>CYP71</i> were differentially expressed in the roots of arbor and shrub willows (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#Fig5">5</a>). Results from <i>Arabidopsis</i> showed that <i>TCTP</i> was expressed throughout the plant, but with the highest levels in meristematic regions of the shoot and root, demonstrating its functions in lateral root development, root development, and root hair cell tip growth. CYP71 is a WD40 domain cyclophilin that functions in gene repression, organogenesis, and meristem development. Based on the above results, <i>TCTP1</i> and <i>CYP71</i> were predicted to be related to the differentiation of arbor and shrub willows.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="The expression levels of TCTP1 and CYP71 in the roots of different arbor and shrub willow varieties."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: The expression levels of TCTP1 and CYP71 in the roots of different arbor and shrub willow varieties.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00424-8/MediaObjects/41438_2020_424_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00424-8/MediaObjects/41438_2020_424_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="857" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>The x-coordinates and y-coordinates represent the willow varieties and relative expression, respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Analysis of chromosome differentiation and genome comparison in <i>S. matsudana</i>
</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec14">Chromosome differentiation in the <i>S. matsudana</i> genome</h4><p>The chromosomes of <i>S. matsudana</i> are displayed in a boxplot according to identity value. Among the 38 chromosomes of <i>S. matsudana</i>, the chromosome group homologous to that of <i>P. trichocarpa</i> was identified and named the A genome, while the remaining 19 chromosomes were classified as the B genome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#MOESM1">S4</a>). The A genome was 252.68âMb in length, with a density of 95 genes per Mb, while the B genome was 278.76âMb in length, with a density of 90 genes per Mb. The densities of both the A and B genomes of <i>S. matsudana</i> were lower than those in <i>P. trichocarpa</i>.</p><p>The collinearity between the genomes of <i>S. matsudana</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> was analyzed by MUMMER software. The results showed that the recombination events occurred between Chr1 in <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, and Chr1A and Chr16A in the A genome of <i>S. matsudana</i>, and similar events were also observed for the B genome of <i>S. matsudana</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#Fig6">6</a>), demonstrating that Chr1 in <i>P. trichocarpa</i> underwent a breakage event. One of the fragments was reconnected with Chr16 and formed Chr1 in <i>S. matsudana</i>, and the other fragment formed Chr1 in <i>S. matsudana</i>, which was consistent with the findings of previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Hou, J. et al. Major chromosomal rearrangements distinguish willow and poplar after the ancestral âSalicoidâ genome duplication. Genome Biol. Evol. 8, 1868â1875 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1630">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Hou, J., Wei, S., Pan, H., Zhuge, Q. &amp; Yin, T. Uneven selection pressure accelerating divergence of Populus and Salix. Hortic. Res. 6, 37 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1633">23</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="The collinearity between the genomes of S. matsudana and P. trichocarpa."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: The collinearity between the genomes of <i>S. matsudana</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00424-8/MediaObjects/41438_2020_424_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00424-8/MediaObjects/41438_2020_424_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="484" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>The <i>x</i>-coordinates and <i>y</i>-coordinates represent the genomes of <i>S. matsudana</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, respectively. Red represents positive alignment, while blue represents reverse alignment</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec15">Comparisons between different genome groups of <i>S. matsudana</i>
</h4><p>Comparisons between different genome groups of <i>S. matsudana</i> showed that the number of genes in the A and B genomes were 23,985 and 25,107, respectively. Collinearity analysis between the A and B genomes revealed different degrees of rearrangement between chromosomes in the A and B genomes, such as between Chr1A and Chr3B, and between Chr1B and Chr3A. In addition, duplications of some chromosome fragments were also observed. For example, Chr8A in the A genome, corresponding to Chr8B in the B genome, was duplicated into Chr10B (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#Fig7">7</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="The collinearity relationships between the A and B genomes of S. matsudana."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 7: The collinearity relationships between the A and B genomes of <i>S. matsudana</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00424-8/MediaObjects/41438_2020_424_Fig7_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00424-8/MediaObjects/41438_2020_424_Fig7_HTML.png" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="680" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p>The <i>x</i>-coordinates and <i>y</i>-coordinates represent the A and B genomes of <i>S. matsudana</i>, respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>PAVs between the A and B genomes of <i>S. matsudana</i> were initially identified by ppsPCP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Qamar, M. T. et al. ppsPCP: a plant presence/absence variants scanner and pan-genome construction pipeline. Bioinformatics 35, 4156â4158 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1733">24</a></sup> software, further verified by searching for PAV sequences in the reference genome, and finally confirmed by comparing the PAVs with queries. The results indicated that the number of PAV mutation-related genes in the A genome compared with the B genome was 19,841, while the number of PAV mutation-related genes in the B genome compared with the A genome was 12,246. GO enrichment analysis of the genes with PAVs between the A and B genomes suggested that the functions of these genes were consistent in rank between the A and B genomes in terms of biological process, cellular component, and molecular function (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#MOESM1">S8</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#MOESM1">S5</a>). For example, most of the genes with PAVs, i.e., 3693 in the A genome and 4231 in the B genome, were annotated as being associated with the metabolic process function in the biological process category, accounting for 54.28% and 55.66% of the total genes in the corresponding A and B genomes, respectively.</p><p>KEGG enrichment analysis of the genes with PAVs between the A and B genomes suggested that nine of the top ten significantly enriched pathways were different between the A and B genomes. The most enriched pathways were the pyruvate metabolic pathway, and the starch and sucrose metabolic pathway in the A and B genomes, with 40 and 100 genes annotated, respectively (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#MOESM1">S6</a>). Significant differences between the A and B genomes suggested their varying roles in regulating basal metabolism.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec16">Genome differentiation of willow</h4><p>A phylogenetic tree was constructed according to the single-copy protein sequences in the five tree species in Salicaceae and the differentiation time of Salicaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Dai, X. et al. The willow genome and divergent evolution from poplar after the common genome duplication. Cell Res. 24, 1274â1277 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1757">13</a></sup>. The differentiation time of the two shrub willows was ~13.3âMYA, while the differentiation of the original species conferring the A and B genomes to <i>S. matsudana</i> started ~25.2âMYA (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#MOESM1">S7</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec17" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec17-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec17">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec17-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Ploidy analysis of willow chromosomes</h3><p>Willows, the generic term for landscaping and energy tree species in Salicaceae, are variable in terms of chromosome ploidy, from 2<i>n</i>â=â38 to 12<i>n</i>â=â228 (refs. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Suda, Y. &amp; Argus, G. W. Chromosome numbers of some North American Salix. Brittonia 20, 191â197 (1968)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1787">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Macalpine, W. J., Shield, I. F., Trybush, S. O., Hayes, C. M. &amp; Karp, A. Overcoming barriers to crossing in willow (Salix spp.) breeding. Asp. Appl. Biol. 90, 173â180 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1790">9</a></sup>). Most shrub willows are diploid, with a genome size of ~400âMb. For instance, the genome sizes of <i>Salix triandra</i> L. (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Thibault, J. Nuclear DNA amount in pure species and hybrid willows (Salix): a flow cytometric investigation. Can. J. Bot. 76, 157â165 (1998)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1797">25</a></sup>) and <i>S. suchowensis</i> Cheng<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Dai, X. et al. The willow genome and divergent evolution from poplar after the common genome duplication. Cell Res. 24, 1274â1277 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1805">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Serapiglia, M. J., Gouker, F. E. &amp; Smart, L. B. Early selection of novel triploid hybrids of shrub willow with improved biomass yield relative to diploids. BMC Plant Biol. 14, 74 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1808">26</a></sup>. are 386 and 425âMb, respectively. Arbor willows are mainly allopolyploid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Argus, G. W. Infrageneric classification of Salix (Salicaceae) in the new world. Syst. Bot. Monogr. 52, 1â121 (1997)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1812">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Barcaccia, G., Meneghetti, S., Albertini, E., Triest, L. &amp; Lucchin, M. Linkage mapping in tetraploid willows: segregation of molecular markers and estimation of linkage phases support an allotetraploid structure for Salix albaÃ Salix fragilis interspecific hybrids. Heredity 90, 169â180 (2003)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1812_1">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Barcaccia, G., Meneghetti, S., Lucchin, M. &amp; De Jong, H. Genetic segregation and genomic hybridization patterns support an allotetraploid structure and disomic inheritance for Salix species. Diversity 6, 633â651 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1815">12</a></sup>, with a genome size of &gt;650âMb. For example, the genome sizes of <i>Salix babylonica</i> L. (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Horjales, M. Cantidades de DNA nuclear en Ã¡rboles y arbustos. Nova Acta Cient. Compostel. (BioloxÃ­a) 13, 23â33 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1822">27</a></sup>) and <i>Salix alba</i> L. (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Thibault, J. Nuclear DNA amount in pure species and hybrid willows (Salix): a flow cytometric investigation. Can. J. Bot. 76, 157â165 (1998)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1830">25</a></sup>) are 748 and 807âMb, respectively. <i>S. matsudana</i> is a tetraploid species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Zhang, M. L. A preliminary cladistic study on the multistaminal willows (Salix) in China. Bull. Bot. Res. 3, 299â305 (1994)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1837">28</a></sup>. A previous study by our group estimated that the genome size of <i>S. matsudana</i> was ~670âMb by flow cytometry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Zhang, J. et al. Determination of chromosome ploidy and genome size of Salix matsudana based on flow cytometry. Jiangsu Agr. Sci. 46, 40â43 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1844">29</a></sup>. In this study, the genome size of this species was 656âMb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#Tab1">1</a>), which was in accordance with the size in the previous study.</p><p>Chromosome doubling of somatic cells and unreduced germ cells are the main pathways for the formation of polyploids in plants during evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Ramsey, J. &amp; Schemske, D. W. Pathways, mechanisms, and rates of polyploid formation in flowering plants. Annu. Rev. Ecol. Syst. 29, 467â501 (1998)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1854">30</a></sup>. Autopolyploids are polyploids derived from the same species, while allopolyploids are polyploids originating from different species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Doyle, J. J. &amp; Egan, A. N. Dating the origins of polyploidy events. N. Phytol. 186, 73â85 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1858">31</a></sup>. For example, hexaploid bread wheat (<i>Triticum aestivum</i> L., AABBDD) originated initially from a hybridization event between <i>Triticum urartu</i> (AA) and <i>T. aestivum</i> L. (BB) to form the tetraploid <i>Triticum turgidum</i> (AABB), followed by the hybridization event between <i>Aegilops tauschii</i> (DD) and <i>T. turgidum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Haider, N. The origin of the B-genome of bread wheat (Triticum aestivum L.). Russ. J. Genet. 49, 263â274 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1880">32</a></sup>. The origin of tetraploid <i>Gossypium hirsutum</i> L. (AADD) and <i>Gossypium barbadense</i> L. (AADD) was dated back to 1.5âMYA, which involved the formation of the A genome donated by <i>Gossypium herbaceum</i> L. or <i>Gossypium arboreum</i> L., and the hybridization event between the A genome and D genome (donated by <i>Gossypium raimondii</i> Ulbrich)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Sattler, M. C., Carvalho, C. R. &amp; Clarindo, W. R. The polyploidy and its key role in plant breeding. Planta 243, 281â296 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1900">33</a></sup>. The genome comparison in the present study suggested that 19 of the 38 total chromosomes in <i>S. matsudana</i> were consistent with those in the <i>P. trichocarpa</i> genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#Fig6">6</a>), indicating that <i>S. matsudana</i> might have originated from hybridization between the ancestors of <i>P. trichocarpa</i> and another species. 4DTv combined transposon analysis predicted that the genome of <i>S. matsudana</i> doubled ~4âMYA (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#Fig3">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#Fig4">4</a>), indicating that the formation of the allotetraploid in this species might have occurred during this period.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Split between arbor and shrub willows</h3><p>Arbors and shrubs are two growth forms specific to tree species. Different types of arbors and shrubs have been observed simultaneously in some willow species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Tu, Z. Y. Willow Breeding And Cultivation (Jiangsu Science &amp; Technology Press, Nanjing, 1982)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1937">34</a></sup>. In this study, the split between the two shrub willow species <i>S. suchowensis</i> and <i>S. purpure</i>a was estimated to have occurred ~13.3âMYA, while the split between the species donating the A genome in <i>S. matsudana</i> and shrub willow occurred ~19.0âMYA (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#MOESM1">S6</a>). However, because of the limited number of willow genomes sequenced, the exact split time between arbor and shrub willows needs to be further studied.</p><p>Arbor and shrub willows displayed significant phenotypic variations in terms of root, branch, and crown growth. The differences in root growth between arbor and shrub willows have been rarely studied, using molecular biology methods. In the current study, <i>TCTP1</i> and <i>CYP71</i>, screened from rapidly evolving genes of the two shrub willows in comparison to <i>S. matsudana</i>, were identified as differentially expressed genes between the roots of arbor and shrub willow varieties. It was reported that <i>TCTP1</i> functioned in lateral root development and determined total root length in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Branco, R. &amp; Masle, J. Systemic signalling through translationally controlled tumour protein controls lateral root formation in Arabidopsis. J. Exp. Bot. 70, 3927â3940 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1970">35</a></sup>. In addition, <i>TCTP</i> induces the formation of adventitious roots in tobacco<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Toscano-Morales, R., Xoconostle-CÃ¡zares, B., MartÃ­nez-Navarro, A. C. &amp; Ruiz-Medrano, R. AtTCTP2 mRNA and protein movement correlates with formation of adventitious roots in tobacco. Plant Signal Behav. 11, e1071003 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1978">36</a></sup>. <i>CYP71</i> functions in gene repression and organogenesis, and abnormalities in <i>CYP71</i> result in the ectopic activation of homologous genes regulating meristem development. Mutants of <i>CYP71</i> displayed phenotypes of low apical meristem activity and disrupted root growth<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Li, H. et al. A WD40 domain cyclophilin interacts with histone H3 and functions in gene repression and organogenesis in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell. 19, 2403â2416 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d32769e1991">37</a></sup>. <i>TCTP1</i> and <i>CYP71</i> were highly expressed in the shrub willow varieties, but weakly expressed in the arbor willow varieties (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#Fig5">5</a>), suggesting that they might have been involved in the split between arbor and shrub willows. Further functional analysis of these genes would provide insight into the nature of the split between arbor and shrub willows.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Differentiation time between the A and B genomes of <i>S. matsudana</i>
</h3><p>Most species have undergone &gt;2 whole-genome duplications (WGDs), which facilitated their survival and adaptation to different environmental conditions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Yang, H. M. GENOMICS (China Science Press, 2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2017">38</a></sup>. The results from this study indicated that species in Salicaceae had undergone at least two WGD events, and the second WGD occurred 36.7â59.7âMYA (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#Fig3">3</a>).</p><p>Fossil records and genome sequences are commonly used to estimate the time of species differentiation. Poplar and willow belong to the same family but different genera. Previous studies based on fossil records revealed that the differentiation of Salicaceae occurred ~45â48âMYA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Boucher, L. D., Manchester, S. R. &amp; Judd, W. S. An extinct genus of Salicaceae based on twigs with attached flowers, fruits, and foliage from the Eocene Green River Formation of Utah and Colorado, USA. Am. J. Bot. 90, 1389â1399 (2003)." href="#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2027">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Manchester, S. R., Judd, W. S. &amp; Handley, B. Foliage and fruits of early poplars (Salicaceae: Populus) from the Eocene of Utah, Colorado, and Wyoming. Int. J. Plant Sci. 167, 897â908 (2006)." href="#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2027_1">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Wu, J. et al. Phylogeny of Salix subgenus Salix sl (Salicaceae): delimitation, biogeography, and reticulate evolution. BMC Evol. Biol. 15, 31 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2030">41</a></sup>. The differentiation time of Salicaceae was estimated to be 12 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Hohmann, N., Wolf, E. M., Lysak, M. A. &amp; Koch, M. A. A time-calibrated road map of Brassicaceae species radiation and evolutionary history. Plant Cell. 27, 2770â2784 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2034">42</a></sup>), 48 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Wu, J. et al. Phylogeny of Salix subgenus Salix sl (Salicaceae): delimitation, biogeography, and reticulate evolution. BMC Evol. Biol. 15, 31 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2038">41</a></sup>), and 52âMYA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Dai, X. et al. The willow genome and divergent evolution from poplar after the common genome duplication. Cell Res. 24, 1274â1277 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2042">13</a></sup> based on sequencing results for the chloroplast, plastid, and nuclear genomes of <i>S. suchowensis</i>, respectively. The variation in differentiation time might be caused by differences in sample selection, which could lead to variation in the correction coefficient and even the final result. The differentiation of the A and B genomes of <i>S. matsudana</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#MOESM1">S7</a>) was deduced to be 25.2âMYA based on the split time of Salicaceae estimated by the <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome, as well as the single-copy orthologs within the whole genome.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec21" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec21-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec21">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec21-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Genome sequencing</h3><p>Fresh leaves of <i>S. matsudana</i> were collected in Changjiang town, Rugao city, Jiangsu Province, China, frozen in liquid nitrogen immediately, and stored at â80âÂ°C for subsequent genomic DNA extraction and library construction. The genomic DNA was fragmented by g-TUBE, repaired, ligated with a dumbbell-shaped adaptor, and digested with exonuclease. The target fragments were screened by BluePippin and used for library construction. Second-generation sequencing was performed according to the protocols provided by the Illumina company. Assembly of the genome sequences from the third-generation sequencing was conducted by the PacBio technique. All sequencing procedures were performed by the BioMarker Technologies Company (Beijing, China).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Genome annotation</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec24">Annotation of repeated sequences</h4><p>The repeated sequence database of the <i>S. matsudana</i> genome was constructed initially based on the structure prediction and de novo prediction, using LTR FINDER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265âW268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2090">43</a></sup>, MITE-Hunter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Han, Y. &amp; Wessler, S. R. MITE-Hunter: a program for discovering miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements from genomic sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, e199 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2094">44</a></sup>, RepeatScout<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; Pevzner, P. A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21, i351âi358 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2098">45</a></sup>, and PILER-DF<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Edgar, R. C. &amp; Myers, E. W. PILER: identification and classification of genomic repeats. Bioinformatics 21, i152âi158 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2102">46</a></sup> software. The database was classified with PASTEClassifier<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Wicker, T. et al. A unified classification system for eukaryotic transposable elements. Nat. Rev. Genet. 8, 973â982 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2107">47</a></sup> and combined with the Repbase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462â467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2111">48</a></sup> database to form the final repeated sequence database. The repeated sequences were annotated based on the final repeated sequence database using RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Tarailo Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Prot. Bioinformatics. 25, 4â10 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2115">49</a></sup> software.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec25">Gene expression</h4><p>Three strategies, namely, de novo prediction, homologous species-based prediction, and unigene-based prediction, were adopted for gene prediction. The prediction results from different strategies were integrated by EVM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2127">50</a></sup> software. GENSCAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Burge, C. &amp; Karlin, S. Prediction of complete gene structures in human genomic DNA. J. Mol. Biol. 268, 78â94 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2131">51</a></sup>, Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction with a hidden Markov model and a new intron submodel. Bioinformatics 19, i215âi225 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2135">52</a></sup>, GlimmerHMM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Majoros, W. H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878â2879 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2139">53</a></sup>, GeneID<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Blanco, E., Parra, G. &amp; GuigÃ³, R. Using geneid to identify genes. Curr. Prot. Bioinformatics. 18, 3â4 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2143">54</a></sup>, and SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2148">55</a></sup> were used for de novo prediction. <i>P. trichocarpa</i> and <i>P. euphratica</i> were selected for homologous species-based prediction using GeMoMa<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Keilwagen, J. et al. Using intron position conservation for homology-based gene prediction. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, e89 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2158">56</a></sup>. PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Campbell, M. A., Haas, B. J., Hamilton, J. P., Mount, S. M. &amp; Buell, C. R. Comprehensive analysis of alternative splicing in rice and comparative analyses with Arabidopsis. BMC Genomics. 7, 327 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2162">57</a></sup> was used for unigene-based prediction.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec26">Annotation of gene functions</h4><p>The predicted gene sequences were subjected to BLAST<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Altschul, S. F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. W. &amp; Lipman, D. J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403â410 (1990)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2174">58</a></sup> alignment with the NR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Marchler-Bauer, A. et al. CDD: a Conserved Domain Database for the functional annotation of proteins. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, D225âD229 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2178">59</a></sup>, COG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Tatusov, R. L. et al. The COG database: new developments in phylogenetic classification of proteins from complete genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 29, 22â28 (2001)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2182">60</a></sup>, and KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Kanehisa, M. &amp; Goto, S. KEGG: kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 27â30 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2186">61</a></sup> functional databases. Functional annotations in the GO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Dimmer, E. C. et al. The UniProt-GO annotation database in 2011. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D565âD570 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2190">62</a></sup> database were compared with the NR database alignment result using Blast2GO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Conesa, A. et al. Blast2GO: a universal tool for annotation, visualization and analysis in functional genomics research. Bioinformatics 21, 3674â3676 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2195">63</a></sup>. In addition, the predicted genes were also subjected to COG, KEGG, and GO enrichment analyses.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Comparative genomic analysis</h3><p>The protein sequences of <i>S. matsudana</i> were aligned with those in related species, including <i>S. suchowensis</i>, <i>S. purpurea</i>, <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, and <i>P. euphratica</i>, to analyze gene duplication within species, gene evolution between species and the classification of species-specific genes. Clustering analysis of gene families was conducted by OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2224">64</a></sup> software. The 4DTv mutation rate was calculated for each of the homologous gene pairs between and within species. LTR sequences with scores â¥6 were detected in the genome by LTR FINDER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265âW268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2228">43</a></sup> and PS SCAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Dan, S. P. SIGNAL SCAN: a computer program that scans DNA sequences for eukaryotic transcriptional elements. Comput. Appl. Bio. 7, 203 (1991)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2232">65</a></sup>, but the repeated sequences detected by LTR FINDER were filtered. The flanking sequences of LTRs were extracted and aligned with MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792â1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d32769e2236">66</a></sup>. The degrees of sequence differences were calculated by DistMat software, with the Kimura model and a 7.3âÃâ10<sup>â9</sup> molecular clock.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec28">qRT-PCR of <i>CYP71</i> and <i>TCTP1</i>
</h3><p>The branches of â2345â (<i>S. suchowensis</i>âÃâ<i>S. integra</i>), â51-5â (<i>S. alberti</i>âÃâ<i>S. leucopithecia</i>), â2521â (<i>S. purpurea</i>), â52-2â (<i>S. suchowensis</i>âÃâ<i>S. leucopithecia</i>), <i>S. integra, S. babylonica</i>, âYanliu 1â (<i>S. psammophila</i>), â795â (<i>S. matsudana</i>âÃâ<i>S. alba</i>), â287â and âYanjiangâ (<i>S. matsudan</i>a) were collected and clipped to a length of 10âcm and diameter of 1âcm. Three biological replicates with ten cuttings per replicate were analyzed for each variety. After 15 days of hydroponic culture, the roots of each cutting were collected and stored at â80âÂ°C. Total RNA from the roots of the ten varieties was extracted using an RNAprep Pure Plant kit (Tiangen). The expression levels of the two genes (<i>CYP71</i> and <i>TCTP1</i>) in each biological replicate were measured by qRT-PCR using three technological replicates. A PrimeScript<sup>TM</sup> RT Reagent Kit (Takara) was used for reverse transcription of the RNAs. Reverse transcription was performed at 37âÂ°C for 30âmin and 85âÂ°C for 5âs. A One-Step SYBR Primer Script PLUS RT-PCR Kit (Takara) was used for qRT-PCR of the <i>CYP71</i> and <i>TCTP1</i> genes. The reaction was performed at 95âÂ°C for 2âmin, followed by 40 cycles of 95âÂ°C for 5âs, 56âÂ°C for 30âs, and 72âÂ°C for 20âs. The <i>Actin</i> gene in <i>Salix</i> was used as the reference gene. The qRT-PCR primers are listed in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00424-8#MOESM1">S9</a>. Relative expression was measured using the 2<sup>âÎÎCt</sup> method. The expression level of â287â (<i>S. matsudana</i>) was selected as the reference level (with a relative expression of 1.0).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec29">Construction of the phylogenetic tree</h3><p>The A and B genomes of <i>S. matsudana</i>, and the genomes of <i>S. suchowensis</i>, <i>S. purpurea</i>, <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, and <i>P. euphratica</i> were used for phylogenetic analysis. The sequence of each single-copy gene family was aligned with MAFFT (v7.205), while the regions with significant differences were removed by Gblocks (v0.91b). Finally, supergenes were obtained by connecting all the aligned gene families in each species. ModelFinder in IQ-TREE was used for model testing, and the best model was predicted to be JTTâ+âFâ+âIâ+âG4. The maximum likelihood method was used to construct a rooted phylogenetic tree. <i>P. trichocarpa</i> was selected as the outgroup species, and the number of bootstraps was set to 1000. Split times and the gradient and Hessian parameters were calculated by MCMCTREE in PAML (v4.9i) software. Visualization of the phylogenetic tree with split times was performed by MCMCTreeR.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec30">Collinearity analysis between the A and B genomes of <i>S. matsudana</i>
</h3><p>The similar gene pairs, with the criteria of an <i>E</i> valueâ&lt;â1eâââ5 and a <i>C</i> scoreâ&gt;â0.5 (<i>C</i> scores were filtered by JCVI software), were identified by comparing gene sequences between species using Diamond (v0.9.29.130). gff3 was used to determine whether similar gene pairs were adjacent in the genome.</p></div></div></section>
